# Cross-model audit — final adjudicated review

**Date:** 2026-07-16 · **Repos:** Agent-Architecture, Agent-Vault, Agent-Overlay
**Reviewers:** Fable 5 (17 unit finders, high effort) and Codex GPT-5.6-Sol (6 area finders, high effort), each blind to the other in round 1, each adversarially cross-reviewing the other's findings in round 2.
**Judge:** one fresh Opus agent per finding (high effort), blinded to authorship (neutral IDs, "Reviewer A/B" framing), required to re-open the cited file before ruling.

## Verdict summary

| | Count |
|---|---|
| Findings judged | 201 (136 Fable + 65 Codex) |
| **Confirmed** | **177** |
| Needs-fix (real issue, claim corrected) | 22 |
| Rejected (false positive) | 2 |

Surviving findings by severity: **9 high**, 64 med, 126 low.
By category: stale-doc 75 · security 36 · DRY 53 · dead-code 35.
By repo: Agent-Architecture 26 · Agent-Overlay 120 · Agent-Vault 53.
Cross-review stances received: 158 agree · 21 refine · 2 refute · 20 unrebutted.

| 1 | F:arch-vs-vault:1 | Fable | Agent-Architecture | openapi-contracts.md:55 | stale-doc | high | Confirmed | The doc tells integrators to fetch the console spec anonymously ('curl http://127.0.0.1:4173/openapi.yaml (Vault) or :4180 (console)', and lines 29-31: the console 'serves its own spec... build against a running server without leaving it'), and frames console.yaml as a loopback 'operator API' with no auth scheme — all pre-Phase-9. | Every load-bearing element of the finding checks out against the code. openapi-contracts.md:55-56 tells integrators to fetch the console spec anonymously (`curl ... :4180`), and lines 29-31 promise you can "build against a running server without leaving it," while the only auth caveat in the doc (lines 33-36) narrowly covers just the approval-decision POST. But in Agent-Overlay crates/overlay-console/src/auth.rs:213-221, a Wire-transport request in non-Open mode that is neither `GET /api/health` nor an Operator falls through and requires a bearer token, returning 403 (OPERATOR_REQUIRED_MESSAGE) otherwise. server.rs:122-124 wraps the dispatch (`Router::new().fallback(dispatch).layer(identity).layer(guard)`), and server.rs:424-458 serves `/openapi.yaml`, `/openapi.json`, `/docs` inside that same guarded dispatch — so those three paths are gated too. The packaged desktop app sets this mode: apps/desktop/src-tauri/src/lib.rs:368 = `wire_access: WireAccess::Restricted`. Thus an anonymous `curl :4180/openapi.yaml` (and /openapi.json, /docs) returns 403 in the shipping app, contradicting the doc. Separately, openapi/console.yaml:4-5 is now `version: 1.0.0-beta.2`, `summary: The local operator and scoped integration API` with declared bearer security and an off-by-default integration plane — none of which the index's "operator API" framing (line 26) mentions. Git confirms staleness: the doc's last commit is 58c746c (2026-07-09) and the curl line dates to 9705a927 (2026-07-02), both predating all Phase 9 shipping commits (transport split, auth spine, tokens, scopes, contract publish). | Update openapi-contracts.md to reflect Phase 9. In the "Consume this in any language" step 1 (line 55-56), note that anonymous spec fetch works for Vault (:4173) but the console (:4180) in the packaged desktop app runs restricted wire access — only `GET /api/health` is anonymous, so `/openapi.yaml`, `/openapi.json`, and `/docs` (and all other routes) require an operator token or a valid scoped integration bearer token; point readers at the committed `console.yaml` file for offline generation. Also revise the console row/framing (line 26 and lines 29-40) from bare "operator API" to "local operator + scoped integration API" matching console.yaml 1.0.0-beta.2, mentioning the off-by-default integration plane and bearer security rather than implying open loopback access. Keep Vault's description unchanged (no such restriction was verified there). |
| 2 | C:cx-overlay-services:1 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/agent-runner/src/watchers/http.rs:260 | security | high | Confirmed | Unauthenticated HTTP triggers are vulnerable to browser-origin request forgery against the loopback daemon. | Read /Users/seanmclaren/Developer/Agent-Overlay/crates/agent-runner/src/watchers/http.rs directly. When a trigger has no auth, line 260 yields auth=None and line 261 yields secret=None; the misconfig block (263, guarded by `if let Some(auth)`), the header-auth block (290, guarded by `(Some(auth), Some(secret))`), and the hmac block (322, same guard) are all skipped, so handle_request proceeds to fire(state, trigger) at line 348. handle_request (245-350) performs no Origin/Host/Referer/Sec-Fetch validation — a grep confirms the only request-header reads are header_value() for the auth header at 297/329, and the "host"/"referer" strings at 447/454 belong to the NODE_SINGLETON_HEADERS folding constant, not any check. The server binds DEFAULT_HOST=127.0.0.1 (line 81, bound at 181-183), so a browser page on the operator's machine can issue a CSRF simple request (or DNS-rebind) to the loopback port and cause a fire with no cross-origin defense. Both reviewers' factual claims match the code. The exposure is correctly scoped to triggers the operator opted to leave unauthenticated; header/hmac triggers remain protected — but that scoping is inherent in the word "unauthenticated" and does not correct the claim. High severity is appropriate for a loopback daemon that fires agent actions. | Add Origin/Host validation to handle_request as defense-in-depth applied to ALL triggers (including authless ones), before fire() at line 348. Reject requests bearing a browser Origin header, or an Origin/Host that is not an allowlisted local value, with 403 — this closes both browser CSRF (Origin present on cross-site fetch) and DNS-rebinding (Host mismatch) against the loopback port. This is the standard hardening for local HTTP daemons and should not depend on the operator having configured trigger auth. Optionally document that authless triggers are intended for trusted local non-browser callers only. |
| 3 | C:cx-overlay-services:5 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-cli/src/commands/serve.rs:55 | security | high | Confirmed | HTTP MCP can be bound beyond loopback without authentication. | The claim is real as stated. program.rs:165-166 exposes a user-supplied `--host` flag (default 127.0.0.1) with no loopback restriction. serve.rs:55 forwards it verbatim into server_options.host, and http.rs:102 binds TcpListener directly to it, so `--host 0.0.0.0` binds beyond loopback. The only gate on the /mcp route is a single route_layer(guard) = HostGuardLayer (http.rs:121-129). HostGuardService::call (http.rs:219-252) performs exact-string Host membership (222-236) plus optional Origin membership (237-249) — DNS-rebinding protection only. There is no token/bearer/credential check anywhere in the HTTP MCP path; the router has no other middleware. Worse, allowed_hosts defaults to default_allowed_hosts(&host, actual_port) (http.rs:110-113, 150-162), which derives the allow-list from the bind host itself, so any network client that reaches the routable address and sends the matching Host header passes the sole gate and reaches enabled MCP resources/tools unauthenticated. Both reviewers' citations are accurate. | Do not rely on the Host/Origin guard as an access-control boundary. Either (1) restrict the `--host` bind to loopback addresses (reject non-loopback hosts unless an explicit opt-in is given), and/or (2) require an authentication credential (bearer token) on the HTTP MCP path whenever the server is bound to a non-loopback address, rejecting unauthenticated requests before session/tool access. At minimum, document loopback-only as the supported deployment and emit a loud warning (or hard opt-in flag) when a routable host is supplied. The Host/Origin membership check should remain but be treated strictly as DNS-rebinding defense, not authentication. |
| 4 | C:cx-overlay-services:6 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-console/src/config.rs:154 | security | high | Confirmed | The standalone control plane can be exposed beyond loopback while remaining unauthenticated. | All cited code verified. config.rs:155-158 reads OVERLAY_UI_HOST into RuntimeConfig.host independently of wire_access (default DEFAULT_HOST=127.0.0.1 at config.rs:27). config.rs:176-178 parse_wire_access maps None (unset) to WireAccess::Open. auth.rs:215-221: RequestIdentityService::call short-circuits `return Box::pin(self.inner.call(request))` whenever `self.wire_access == WireAccess::Open`, before the bearer/integration-token path at 223+. server.rs:101-119 create_overlay_server takes host and wire_access as independent options with no interlock forcing Restricted on a non-loopback bind. The sole host-based check, LocalRequestGuardLayer (origin_guard.rs:112-126, allowlist built at 189-196), is DNS-rebinding protection only: it validates the attacker-controllable Host header against an allowlist that always includes localhost/127.0.0.1 and also includes the configured host verbatim, and never filters by source IP — so it is not an authentication barrier. Therefore binding beyond loopback (OVERLAY_UI_HOST=0.0.0.0 or a LAN IP) while wire_access stays at its Open default yields an unauthenticated, network-reachable control plane. The claim is stated conditionally and does not misrepresent the safe loopback default; severity is appropriate given the control plane can launch processes and drive approvals. | Couple the two knobs so an unauthenticated plane cannot be exposed off-host. At config/startup, when the resolved bind host is not a loopback address, either refuse to start unless wire_access is explicitly Restricted, or force Restricted (require an integration bearer token) with a loud warning. Alternatively/additionally, change the WireAccess default to Open only when host is loopback and Restricted otherwise. Do not rely on LocalRequestGuardLayer for this — it is DNS-rebinding protection and is Host-header spoofable, not source-IP access control. Add a regression test asserting that a non-loopback host with default wire_access rejects unauthenticated wire /api requests. |
| 5 | C:cx-overlay-services:7 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-console/src/sse.rs:49 | security | high | Confirmed | Revoked, disabled, or workspace-mismatched integration tokens retain existing global SSE subscriptions. | The code confirms the claim at both cited locations. SseHub (sse.rs:49-51) stores only `Vec<UnboundedSender<SseEvent>>` — no token, identity, or workspace is retained with a subscription. `broadcast` (sse.rs:68-73) fans each event to every stored sender and drops a client only when a send fails (i.e. the client already disconnected); it never re-checks authorization. `subscribe`/`open_event_stream` (sse.rs:77-113) register the sender and stream indefinitely with no revalidation. Authorization is a strictly per-request middleware pass (auth.rs:241-274): `integrations.resolve(&bearer, active_workspace)` decides disabled/invalid/Revoked/WorkspaceMismatch/missing_scope at dispatch time, and for `/api/events` (scope EventsSubscribe, auth.rs:131-136) that runs exactly once at connect. Consequently a token revoked, disabled, or rendered workspace-mismatched (via an active-workspace switch) after the stream opens keeps receiving all broadcast events until the client disconnects. Note the hub is also fully global — every connected client receives every event with no per-workspace filtering even at connect — so the exposure is at least as broad as claimed. | Add liveness/authorization revalidation to long-lived SSE streams instead of relying solely on the connect-time middleware check. Concretely: (1) tag each hub subscription with its resolved integration identity (token_id + scoped workspace) rather than storing a bare sender; (2) on each broadcast (or on a periodic tick alongside the 15s heartbeat) re-resolve the token against current integration state and active workspace, and drop/close any subscription that now resolves to Revoked/Invalid/disabled/WorkspaceMismatch/missing-scope; and (3) proactively close matching streams when a token is revoked/disabled or the active workspace changes (event-driven invalidation via the integrations/controller state). Also consider per-subscription workspace filtering at broadcast time so a stream only ever receives events for the workspace its token is scoped to, closing the global-fan-out gap. Add a regression test asserting a subscribed client stops receiving events after its token is revoked or the active workspace switches. |
| 6 | C:cx-overlay-core:1 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-mcp/src/tools/custom.rs:1035 | security | high | Confirmed | `bash:<command>` approval gates can be bypassed by compound or wrapped shell commands | The code at custom.rs:1035-1071 matches the claim exactly. `shell_command_names` builds its list from only two sources: `basename(command)` (1036), and — for an `sh/bash/zsh -c` invocation — the FIRST whitespace token of the `-c` string (`first_shell_command_name` splits on `split_whitespace().next()`, 1067). `shell_approval_requirement` (917-929) is the sole shell gate check and matches `bash:<name>` gates by exact equality against that list (line 924: `command_names.iter().any(\|name\| name == value)`). It is invoked on both the gate-probe path (329) and the execute path (384); no other shell gating exists. Therefore `sh -c 'echo ok; rm -rf x'` produces names ["sh","echo"], and a `bash:rm` gate never fires while the full compound command executes. The same under-gating holds for pipes, `;`, `&&`, and for `env`/`xargs`/`sh script.sh` wrappers (the latter yields None from `inline_shell_command` at 1053-1058 since there is no `-c`, so only the outer basename is checked). Only `bash:*` (line 924) matches reliably. Reviewer A's core claim and Reviewer B's expansion are both borne out by the code. High severity is appropriate for a security approval gate whose named form is trivially evadable. | Stop treating the first shell token as the command identity. Options, in order of robustness: (1) parse the `-c` string into its full set of invoked command names (handling `;`, `&&`, `\|\|`, `\|`, subshells, and common wrappers like `env`/`xargs`/`sudo`/`nice`) and match every extracted name against `bash:<name>` gates; or (2) fail closed — when a shell command contains compound-command metacharacters or an inline `-c` string that cannot be fully decomposed, require approval (or deny) rather than gating on the first token alone. At minimum, document that only `bash:*` is a trustworthy gate and treat named `bash:<cmd>` gates as advisory until decomposition is implemented. Add regression tests covering `sh -c 'echo ok; rm -rf x'`, piped/`&&`-chained commands, and wrapper forms. |
| 7 | C:cx-overlay-services:4 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-cli/src/commands/migrate.rs:379 | security | high | Needs-fix | Migration rollback can write outside the workspace through a symlinked destination component. **[corrected: Real issue, but severity corrected from high to low (defense-in-depth hardening, not an exploitable high-severity vulnerability). Corrected claim: 'Migration rollback restore lacks canonical/symlink containment, so a symlinked destination-path component can redirect a write outside the workspace — but only given pre-existing write access to the user's own local workspace, which crosses no privilege boundary.' Location (migrate.rs:377-386 restore loop + 460-464 lexical check) is correct.]** | The code facts are accurate. is_safe_relative_path (crates/overlay-cli/src/commands/migrate.rs:460-464) is a purely lexical check (empty / leading-'/' / literal '..' components) with no symlink resolution. rollback_latest_backup (377-386) does workspace_path.join(file) then create_dir_all(parent) (382) and atomic_write (384) with no canonical-containment check, and atomic_write (crates/overlay-core/src/loaders/atomic_write.rs:54-65) writes its temp file into file_path.parent() before renaming — so a symlinked parent component pointing to an existing directory outside the workspace does redirect the write. The escape mechanism is genuine. But the 'high security' grading overstates it. The backup store is local (.cache/migrations/backups/ inside the workspace) and the manifest is generated by the tool itself from to_posix_relative of files under workspace.root (create_migration_backup, 308-340) — never untrusted input. Exploitation requires an attacker to either forge a manifest in the backup dir or plant a symlink at a manifest path's parent inside the workspace; both presuppose pre-existing write access to the user's own workspace. This is a local single-user CLI running as the invoking user over that user's own corpus, so the symlink 'escape' grants no capability the attacker doesn't already have and crosses no privilege boundary. Reviewer B's own caveat ('exploitation presupposes a planted symlink or attacker-controlled backup content') is precisely why this is not high. There is no untrusted-remote-archive vector as in classic zip-slip/tar-symlink attacks. | Add canonical containment to the rollback restore loop: after computing destination_path = workspace_path.join(file), resolve the parent (fs::canonicalize on the existing parent, or reject any component that is a symlink via symlink_metadata) and verify the resolved path is still a prefix of a canonicalized workspace_path before calling create_dir_all/atomic_write; error out (invalid_manifest-style) otherwise. Apply the same symlink-refusing check symmetrically on the write side in create_migration_backup (316-327) for consistency. Track as a low-priority hardening item, not a release-blocking security fix. |
| 8 | C:cx-overlay-core:2 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-mcp/src/resources/memory.rs:220 | security | high | Needs-fix | Memory resources can expose files outside the workspace through symlinks **[corrected: Downgrade to medium (high only if opening untrusted third-party workspaces is a supported use case). Corrected claim: the MCP memory resource read path (memory.rs:220-243) bypasses overlay-core's post-symlink containment convention, so a symlink planted inside a workspace layer by a workspace author is followed to any readable host file. It is an internal-consistency/hardening gap reachable only by someone with workspace write access, not a client-controlled path-traversal exposure.]** | see above | Route the memory resource reads through overlay-core's canonical containment helpers instead of raw std::fs. Make read_layered_optional_text (and read_layered_optional_yaml_list) canonicalize the resolved file and assert it is inside the layer root before reading — mirroring read_optional_contained_text — so the MCP memory path matches the containment guarantee already enforced elsewhere in overlay-core. Track as a medium-severity hardening fix. |
| 9 | C:cx-vault-rust:1 | Codex | Agent-Vault | crates/vault-server/src/native_chat.rs:273 | security | high | Needs-fix | Native Chat settings can exfiltrate process-environment secrets or the stored provider key to an arbitrary endpoint. **[corrected: Downgrade to low/informational. Accurate claim: because api_key_env is unconstrained, a caller that already controls the loopback-gated, CSRF-protected /api/native-chat/settings surface could name an arbitrary process-environment variable and have its value sent as a Bearer token to an operator-chosen http(s) base_url. This is a local hardening gap, not a remote/high-severity exfiltration path — the origin guard blocks browser/remote callers, redirect::none() prevents token leakage via redirect, and arbitrary base_url is the intended bring-your-own OpenAI-compatible-provider behavior.]** | Code mechanism confirmed exactly as described: native_chat.rs:273-289 constrains base_url only to http/https scheme (host unbounded); :302-314 accepts any non-empty api_key_env; native_chat_api_key (:2622) reads it via std::env::var; call_openai_compatible_chat_with_client (:2210) sends `Bearer {api_key}` to the configured URL. However, both provider clients set redirect::Policy::none() (:2190, :2244), and the settings route (server.rs:297) sits under the whole-app LocalRequestGuardLayer (server.rs:186/217) whose check (origin_guard.rs:172-226) enforces the loopback Host allowlist, foreign-Origin CSRF rejection, and application/json content-type. There is therefore no remote or cross-origin path to set these fields — writing them requires control of the loopback-gated settings surface. The base_url freedom is the intended bring-your-own-provider design. The real residual is that the unbounded api_key_env lets a local settings write read an unrelated process-env secret and ship it to a chosen endpoint. That warrants low/informational hardening, not high severity, and the claim needs the loopback-gating qualification. | Keep as a low-severity hardening item. Optionally constrain api_key_env to an allowlist/prefix (e.g. reject names that don't match the expected provider key pattern) so a settings write cannot repurpose the field to read unrelated process-env secrets. No remote-facing fix is required; the loopback guard and redirect::none() already close the browser/remote and redirect vectors. |
| 10 | C:cx-arch:5 | Codex | Agent-Architecture | acceptance/README.md:14 | stale-doc | med | Confirmed | The documented build-then-run capture acceptance path omits its requirement for an overlay executable already installed on PATH. | The automated-run quickstart (acceptance/README.md:14-23) lists only three cargo builds plus `node capture-triage-loop.mjs`, yet a green run with the all-Rust defaults genuinely requires an `overlay` executable already on PATH. Traced end-to-end: (1) the default `overlayCmd=[overlayBin]` is a one-element native path, so `overlayCliPathFor` (capture-triage-loop.mjs:93-97) returns undefined and `OVERLAY_CLI_PATH` is deleted and never re-set (lines 142-146). (2) In the adapter (triage-capture-harness.mjs:90-111), unset `OVERLAY_CLI_PATH` skips branch 1; branch 2's `repoRoot/packages/cli/dist/index.js` resolves relative to the copied /tmp workspace (cp at line 109) and cannot exist — and `packages/cli` was in fact deleted (git commit b918886, "delete packages/cli … R1 cutover"; the directory is absent). So it falls to spawning bare `"overlay"` from PATH (lines 108-111). (3) The adapter cannot learn `target/debug/overlay` any other way: execute_binary_adapter builds the child env from spawned_agent_env (execution.rs:566), whose SPAWNED_AGENT_ENV_ALLOWLIST (lines 60-101) passes PATH but not OVERLAY_CLI_PATH; the `input.overlay_cli_path` field is consumed only to write MCP config for claude-code/codex agents (write_claude_mcp_config:845, prepare_codex_execution:912), never injected into a deterministic binary adapter. So `cargo build` alone (which places the binary at target/debug/overlay, not on PATH) leaves the executor's bare-`overlay` spawn to ENOENT unless a native install put `overlay` on PATH. The requirement is stated in the manual procedure (:42-43) and knobs section (:133-136) but omitted from the quickstart the finding cites. Both reviewers' chains check out against the code; med severity is apt because the same README documents the behavior elsewhere. | Add one line to the automated-run quickstart (acceptance/README.md:14-23) making the native default's PATH dependency explicit — e.g. note that the all-Rust default spawns the triage executor's `overlay serve` via a bare `overlay` resolved on PATH, so `overlay` must be installed on PATH (a native install does this; `cargo build` alone only produces target/debug/overlay). Alternatively, cross-link the existing manual step 3 (:42-43) / knobs (:133-136) explanation from the quickstart so it no longer presents as self-contained. Purely a doc fix in Agent-Architecture; no code change required. |

The Overlay code refutes this. apps/desktop/src-tauri/src/lib.rs:863 registers register_asynchronous_uri_scheme_protocol("overlay", ...) and dispatches into an in-process axum router (dispatch_scheme_request); the packaged window navigates to overlay://localhost/... (lib.rs:231/280/1084), and the in-file comment at lib.rs:10 states "The packaged UI dispatches into the same router over overlay://localhost." That is a custom-scheme + in-process-router model, not a loopback origin. Additionally, tauri.conf.json:45 sets externalBin = ["binaries/overlay","binaries/agent-runner"] — agent-overlay-server is not a bundled sidecar; web assets are shipped as resources (../web/dist → web-dist) and served by the in-process router. Reviewer A's cited evidence matches the current tree exactly. | Reword agent-vault.md:64-66 so it no longer says Overlay ships "the same model-B" wrap. State that both repos converge on one desktop-delivery story via cargo-built externalBins, but note the transport differs: Vault loads a loopback origin of a bundled sidecar (model-B), while Overlay's packaged window dispatches into an in-process router over the overlay://localhost custom scheme (agent-overlay-server is not a bundled sidecar; externalBin = overlay + agent-runner). Restrict the standalone model-B loopback-sidecar description to Vault. |
| 13 | F:arch-vs-vault:2 | Fable | Agent-Architecture | build-plan.md:274 | stale-doc | med | Confirmed | The Phase 5 Tauri-wrap record states in present tense that 'Overlay apps/desktop/src-tauri bundles agent-overlay-server plus the runnable overlay CLI, and since Phase 8.2 also bundles agent-runner'. | build-plan.md:273-275 states the Overlay apps/desktop/src-tauri bundle contains `agent-overlay-server` plus the `overlay` CLI (and, since 8.2, `agent-runner`). | 14 | F:arch-vs-overlay:2 | Fable | Agent-Architecture | build-plan.md:1482 | stale-doc | med | Confirmed | The Phase 9 closing status asserts 'All four conditions hold as of Overlay `0c477d7`' and that 'launch routes are governed identically for every caller', which the doc's own 140e7ea/d1a3879 addendum and current code supersede. | The closing status block (build-plan.md:1480-1485) asserts "All four conditions hold as of Overlay `0c477d7`" and "launch routes are governed identically for every caller," but current code contradicts this. | 23 | C:cx-overlay-services:3 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/agent-runner/src/crontab.rs:414 | security | med | Confirmed | A newline in `--state-dir` can inject lines into the managed crontab block. | The code confirms the claimed asymmetry and injection path. In cli.rs the `--state-dir` arm (lines 1341-1343) reads its value with a bare `read_option_value(...)` and no sanitization, whereas the `--runner-command` arm (1352-1363) explicitly rejects `'\n'`/`'\r'` with the comment that the value is "embedded verbatim into single-line unit formats (crontab entries, systemd ExecStart); a line break would smuggle extra entries/directives." The state_dir value flows through `resolve_state_dir` -> `resolve_lexical` (overlay-core/src/internal/fs_util.rs:108-130), which normalizes only `.`/`..`/separators by iterating path components — a newline inside a `Component::Normal` segment is preserved verbatim. It is then rendered by `path_string` (fs_util.rs:59-61), a lossy `to_string_lossy` with no sanitization, and interpolated by `begin_marker`/`end_marker` (crontab.rs:414-420) directly into the single-line comments `# BEGIN agent-runner managed: {}` / `# END ...`, which `managed_block_text` (crontab.rs:422-429) writes into the crontab. On Unix a path component may legally contain a newline, so a `--state-dir` value such as `/tmp/x\n* * * * * <cmd>` splits the marker line and injects an arbitrary crontab entry — the exact class of injection the `--runner-command` guard was written to prevent. The claim, the location (crontab.rs:414), and the med severity all hold. (One trivial imprecision: `path_string`/`to_posix_relative` live in the shared overlay-core crate, not agent-runner's own module, but the cited line numbers match and this does not affect the ruling.) | Apply the same CR/LF rejection to the `--state-dir` arm in cli.rs (1341-1343) that `--runner-command` already uses: after reading the value, return a message error if it contains `'\n'` or `'\r'`. Consider consolidating both checks into a shared `reject_line_breaks(value, arg)` helper so any future flag whose value reaches single-line unit formats (crontab, systemd, launchd) is guarded consistently. Since path_string/begin_marker are the true sink, optionally add a defense-in-depth assertion/sanitization at the marker-formatting layer so the invariant is enforced even if another caller supplies an unchecked state_dir. |
| 24 | C:cx-overlay-services:2 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/agent-runner/src/dispatch.rs:257 | security | med | Confirmed | Webhook floods can create unbounded tasks and queued gate waiters. | Every mechanical assertion in the finding checks out against the code.  1. http.rs `fire` (line 348, defined 536-545) unconditionally `tokio::spawn`s a task for each accepted request. There is a body-size cap (`body_limit_bytes`) and a `request_timeout` in `consume_request` (391-433), but nothing accounts for or caps the number of in-flight accepted requests. Confirmed.  2. The dispatch-gate handler pushes a fresh `oneshot` sender into `state.waiters` on every call — `state.waiters.push(sender)` at dispatch.rs:271. Confirmed.  3. When `debounce_ms > 0` it spawns a sleeping timer task per call — `tokio::spawn(async move { sleep(...).await; ... })` at dispatch.rs:289. Confirmed.  4. The flood-never-flushes claim holds. Each call bumps `next_timer_gen` and sets `state.timer = Some(generation)` (275-277), stranding the prior timer. When a timer wakes it only proceeds if `state.timer == Some(generation)` (294); every generation but the newest fails that check and does nothing. So while requests arrive closer together than `debounce_ms`, the current generation keeps advancing and no timer ever reaches `flush_dispatch_gate`, which is the only place `state.waiters` is drained via `std::mem::take` (355). The waiters vector therefore grows for the whole duration of a sustained flood. Even in the no-debounce path, while one fire is running (`state.running >= max_concurrency`, default 1) additional calls take the `Busy`/`pending` branch (349-351) and their senders remain in `state.waiters` un-taken until the running fire completes and re-flushes. In both paths waiters and their awaiting http tasks accumulate with no upper bound.  This is a genuine unbounded-growth / DoS vector. Mitigating context (does not change the ruling, informs severity): the server binds loopback by default (127.0.0.1:8787, http.rs:1, 81), so off-host floods require the operator to set a non-loopback `host`; and for triggers configured with `header` or `hmac-sha256` auth, unauthorized requests are rejected (300-306, 330-336) before `fire` is reached, so waiter/task accumulation is only reachable for no-auth triggers or by an already-credentialed flooder. med severity is appropriate. Note this is a faithful port of the TS reference (the surrounding comments track "TS ..." line by line), so the same unbounded behavior exists upstream — this is a pre-existing design gap, not a port regression. | Introduce an explicit bound on accepted work. Concretely: (a) cap concurrent in-flight accepted requests in http.rs with a per-server semaphore (or a max-connections limit), returning 503 when exhausted, so `fire` cannot spawn without bound; and/or (b) cap the per-trigger `state.waiters` length in dispatch.rs (e.g. coalesce debounced calls onto a bounded set of senders, or reject with a busy/503 signal once a threshold is exceeded) so a sustained sub-debounce flood cannot grow the vector or its awaiting tasks without limit. Since this mirrors the TS reference, flag the same gap for parity upstream so the two implementations stay aligned. |
| 25 | C:cx-overlay-services:12 | Codex | Agent-Overlay | crates/agent-runner/src/watchers/schedule.rs:311 | DRY | med | Confirmed | Cron validation and execution use independent parsers that have already drifted. | Verified in code. overlay-core is_positive_int (crates/overlay-core/src/schemas/trigger.rs:254-256) validates a cron step with only: non-empty + all-ASCII-digits + parse_number(value) >= 1.0. parse_number (line 250-251) returns value.parse().unwrap_or(f64::NAN); an overflowing digit run parses to Ok(inf), and inf >= 1.0 is true (empirically confirmed), so schema validation ACCEPTS the trigger. There is no finiteness/integer guard. Runner parse_positive_int (crates/agent-runner/src/watchers/schedule.rs:418-430) applies the same digit gate but then line 426 `if !value.is_finite() ... return Err` returns ScheduleError::CronStep for inf; this propagates through parse_cron_expression and lands in the skip path (line 301-306, "Skipping schedule trigger"). So the accepted trigger is REJECTED and silently skipped at execution — a genuine validate-vs-execute drift between two independent parsers. The schedule.rs:340-343 comment even asserts the two sides "behave identically on both sides of the seam," which is the exact parity being violated (the runner faithfully mirrors TS Number.isInteger/finiteness; is_positive_int omits that check). Both reviewers' mechanism and line citations are accurate. Med severity is right: requires a pathological ~300+-digit step, so low real-world likelihood, but a real correctness/DRY defect. | Add the missing finiteness/integer guard to overlay-core is_positive_int so it matches parse_positive_int's contract: reject when the parsed f64 is non-finite (e.g. change the predicate to also require parse_number(value).is_finite(), or route through a shared helper). Better still, eliminate the drift at its root by having both the schema validator and the runner parser call one shared cron-step predicate in overlay-core (the crate both sides already depend on), so validation and execution cannot diverge again. Add a golden/fixture case for an overflowing digit-run step to the cron test table to lock the behavior. |

| 27 | F:overlay-cli:1 | Fable | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-cli/src/commands/init.rs:271 | DRY | med | Confirmed | Tilde-expansion + path-resolution logic is implemented three times in the crate: init.rs resolve_target_path, workspace_discovery.rs resolve_input_path, and serve_logging.rs resolve_log_dir, along with a repeated $HOME-fallback chain. | All three cited sites verified. init.rs:271-283 resolve_target_path and workspace_discovery.rs:112-125 resolve_input_path implement the same tilde-expansion (`~`/`~/` special-casing, `&input[2..]`, `trim_end_matches('/')`) followed by node_posix_resolve — same algorithm and same "TS resolveInputPath / path.resolve" Node-semantics provenance comment; they differ only in param typing (&Path with inline path_string() vs &str), which is why they weren't already unified. serve_logging.rs:95-103 resolve_log_dir re-implements only the `~`/`~/` expansion, deferring resolution to setup_serve_log at 72-75 — exactly as A stated ("without the resolve step"). | 41 | F:overlay-mcp:1 | Fable | Agent-Overlay | crates/overlay-mcp/src/tools/custom.rs:1062 | security | med | Confirmed | The `bash:<command>` approval gate inspects only the first whitespace-delimited token of an inline `sh -c` string, so chained or injected commands (`;`, `&&`, `\|`) execute without triggering the gate — including MCP-caller input that `render_string` substitutes into the inline string with no shell escaping. | The code at the cited locations reproduces the claimed bypass end to end. `first_shell_command_name` (custom.rs:1062-1071) extracts only `trimmed.split_whitespace().next()` (quote-stripped) with zero handling of shell separators (`;`, `&&`, `\|\|`, `\|`, newlines). `shell_command_names` (1035-1046) therefore returns at most `[basename(command), first_inline_token]` — e.g. `["sh","echo"]` for `command=/bin/sh, args=["-c","echo hi; rm -rf ~"]`. `shell_approval_requirement` (917-930) only matches a `bash:<value>` gate when `value=="*"` or some entry of that list equals `value`, so a `bash:rm` gate compares against `["sh","echo"]`, misses, and returns `None`. `render_string` (790-813) does raw part concatenation of the resolved variable with no shell escaping, so MCP-caller input `msg="hi; rm -rf ~"` renders `echo hi; rm -rf ~` into the `-c` argument. `execute_shell_tool` (378-424) then execs `Command::new("/bin/sh").args(["-c","echo hi; rm -rf ~"])`, which sh parses and runs in full; and because the requirement is `None`, the entire approval + effect-hash matching block (385-397) is skipped — no gate fires, no approval is requested, and the effect-hash binding never engages. The one gate that still catches this is the wildcard `bash:*`, which the finding explicitly excludes. Both reviewers' line citations (1035-1071, 790-813, 379-398) are accurate. | Stop gating shell tools on a lexical first-token command name. Either (a) when the outer executable is a shell invoked with `-c`, parse the inline string into all command words across separators (`;`, `&&`, `\|\|`, `\|`, newlines, subshells, backticks/`$()`) and feed every discovered command name into the gate check, or (b) treat any inline `sh -c`/`bash -c`/`zsh -c` payload that contains shell metacharacters or interpolated caller input as requiring approval by default (fail-closed). Additionally, shell-escape/quote variable substitutions in `render_string` for values that land inside inline shell strings, or forbid unescaped template interpolation into `-c` payloads. | 53 | C:cx-vault-rust:2 | Codex | Agent-Vault | crates/vault-server/src/origin_guard.rs:195 | security | med | Confirmed | A non-loopback bind exposes the privileged API without client authentication. | The code confirms the claim as stated. In LocalRequestGuardService::check (origin_guard.rs:172-226) the only gates on an /api/* request are: (1) a Host allowlist (176-188), (2) a foreign-Origin rejection that fires ONLY when an Origin header is present (198-205 — an absent Origin falls through to None), and (3) a content-type == application/json check on body methods (209-223). There is no client secret or token check anywhere in the guard. local_host_allowlist (261-268) unconditionally includes "127.0.0.1:{port}", "localhost:{port}", "[::1]:{port}" AND "{host}:{port}", and build_runtime_config takes HOST verbatim (config.rs:111). The Host header is entirely client-controlled by any non-browser client, so DNS-rebinding protection does nothing against a direct socket client: with HOST bound to 0.0.0.0 (or a LAN address), a LAN attacker opens a TCP connection and sends `Host: 127.0.0.1:PORT` (which is always allowlisted), no Origin header, and `Content-Type: application/json`, satisfying all three checks and reaching every /api handler — including secret, arbitrary-markdown, and subprocess-triggering routes. instance_token is not authentication: grep shows it is only echoed into the /api/health body (server.rs:1399-1401) and never consulted on any request path. The module doc itself (origin_guard.rs:5-8) treats loopback binding as the security boundary, and the default HOST is 127.0.0.1 (config.rs:32), so this exposure requires a deliberate non-loopback bind by the operator — which is exactly the precondition the claim names, so "med" severity (contingent on operator configuration) is appropriate. | Treat non-loopback binding as unsupported unless authenticated. Either (a) refuse to start when HOST resolves to a non-loopback address, logging that the guard's threat model assumes loopback, or (b) if LAN binding is a genuine use case, add a per-request client-auth check (e.g. a required bearer/instance token compared in the guard) so Host-only spoofing no longer grants access. Also add a regression test asserting that an /api/* request with an allowlisted Host but no Origin and no auth is rejected under a non-loopback bind. The build_runtime_config HOST passthrough and the {host}:{port} allowlist entry are the concrete spots to gate. |
| 54 | C:cx-vault-rust:4 | Codex | Agent-Vault | crates/vault-server/src/vault/files.rs:455 | security | med | Confirmed | Vault path containment is vulnerable to a symlink time-of-check/time-of-use race. | Code at files.rs:455-475 confirms the check-then-use-on-lexical-path defect. `resolve_vault_file_path` calls `canonicalize_string(&path)` (line 466) or `nearest_existing_real_path` (line 471) and validates the *canonical* result via `assert_path_inside_vault` (lines 467/472), but returns the lexical join `path` (lines 468/474), not the validated canonical path. `read_vault_file` then reads that lexical path via `tokio::fs::read_to_string(&path)` (line 155), and `write_vault_file` writes it via `atomic_write_vault_file` → `tokio::fs::rename(&temp_path, path)` (line 811). Canonicalize and I/O are distinct `.await` points, so a symlink or intermediate directory swapped between the check and the I/O redirects the read/write outside the vault. A static (non-racing) symlink is caught because canonicalize resolves it before validation, so the defect is precisely a symlink TOCTOU race, exactly as claimed. Location, mechanism, and line references are all accurate. | Make `resolve_vault_file_path` return the canonical path it validated rather than the lexical `path`, and have `read_vault_file`/`write_vault_file` operate on that. For the write/create case where the target does not yet exist, resolve and validate the nearest existing parent canonically, then re-join the remaining non-existent components onto the canonical parent and operate on that. For full robustness against the residual race on intermediate components, prefer handle-based I/O (openat / O_NOFOLLOW on the resolved parent handle) so the opened object cannot be redirected after validation. Keep the severity at medium given exploitation requires a local actor able to concurrently swap symlinks inside the single-user vault directory. |

| 66 | F:overlay-desktop:1 | Fable | Agent-Overlay | apps/desktop/src-tauri/src/lib.rs:89 | security | med | Needs-fix | The single-instance guard uses a predictable socket path in world-writable /tmp with no peer-credential check, letting any local user permanently block app launch or send it commands. **[corrected: On Unix, the single-instance guard uses a predictable, world-accessible socket path (/tmp/com.agentoverlay.desktop.{user}.sock, line 89) and performs no peer-credential check. Any local user who pre-binds that path denies the victim's app launch (persisting even via a stale file, since sticky-bit /tmp blocks removal), and any local user can connect to trigger an unauthenticated show/focus of the main window (the read bytes are ignored). Impact is local DoS on launch plus an unauthenticated window-focus request — not arbitrary command execution. Severity med is reasonable, and the exposure is limited to shared multi-user hosts.]** | Code confirms the core defects. single_instance_socket_path() (line 89) builds a predictable world-readable /tmp path. claim_single_instance() treats any successful connect as an existing instance and exits the launch (notify_existing_instance lines 117-123 → return Ok(false) lines 137-139), so a local attacker who pre-binds that path denies every launch; on the sticky-bit /tmp stale-file path, remove_file fails, bind returns AddrInUse, and the function returns Err — launch fails even with no live attacker process (lines 141-172). The accept loop (lines 182-190) reads 16 bytes into `buffer` and discards them with no peer-credential or message validation, then calls show_main_window. However, show_main_window (lines 769-777) only does window.show()+set_focus(), so the sole reachable effect of an unauthenticated connection is focusing the window — not 'sending commands' as claimed. The real issue is a local launch-denial DoS plus an unauthenticated window-focus poke; 'send it commands' overstates the capability. | Move the socket into a per-user, non-world-writable runtime directory (e.g. $XDG_RUNTIME_DIR on Linux or ~/Library/Application Support on macOS) created with 0700, so other users cannot pre-create or squat the path. Additionally, verify the peer with SO_PEERCRED (getsockopt) and drop connections whose UID does not match the current process, and require the expected "show" message before focusing rather than acting on any connection. This closes both the launch-denial DoS and the unauthenticated focus request. |

| 71 | C:cx-vault-rust:3 | Codex | Agent-Vault | crates/vault-server/src/origin_guard.rs:229 | security | med | Needs-fix | Foreign web pages can embed and probe private vault assets. **[corrected: Foreign web pages can embed private vault assets from the loopback asset origin (127.0.0.1:4183) and detect their existence and dimensions, because AssetRequestGuardService::check ignores Origin and no Cross-Origin-Resource-Policy header is set. This is an existence/embedding oracle, not content exfiltration — cross-origin JS still cannot read the bytes (opaque response / canvas taint). Severity low-to-med, not full med.]** | Code confirms the mechanism but not the "probe content" framing. AssetRequestGuardService::check (origin_guard.rs:230-250) gates asset requests on Host-allowlist + GET/HEAD only and never reads Origin; serve_vault_asset (http.rs:305-330) emits Content-Type, nosniff, an inert CSP, and attachment for active types but no Cross-Origin-Resource-Policy (grep found zero CORP references in the crate). The test asset_guard_rejects_bad_hosts_and_non_read_methods_but_allows_foreign_origins (asset_server.rs:180-189) asserts a foreign Origin https://attacker.example gets 200 with no ACAO. So a foreign page CAN embed a loopback vault asset (<img src=127.0.0.1:4183/assets/...>) and learn existence/dimensions — real. But these are cross-origin image/media embeds: the response is opaque / the canvas is tainted, so cross-origin JS cannot read the bytes. "Probe private vault assets" implies content read, which the browser model prevents. Fix scope is also constrained: config.rs forbids asset_port==port and .env.example ships app=4173 / assets=4183, so the app's own note previews already load assets cross-origin — a CORP: same-origin would break the app. | Add a Cross-Origin-Resource-Policy header to serve_vault_asset, but use CORP: same-site (or cross-origin) rather than same-origin, since the app (4173) legitimately embeds assets from the separate asset port (4183) and same-origin would break note previews. Optionally reject clearly foreign Origin values in AssetRequestGuardService::check while still allowing null/no-Origin and the app origin, to close the existence oracle. Update the test's expectation and the header assertions accordingly. |

| 94 | F:overlay-desktop:2 | Fable | Agent-Overlay | apps/desktop/src-tauri/src/lib.rs:664 | security | low | Confirmed | reap_own_orphan() trusts an unauthenticated /api/health response from whatever process answers on shared loopback port 4180 to choose a same-user PID to SIGTERM/SIGKILL. | Verified against apps/desktop/src-tauri/src/lib.rs. health_response_body() (708) does an unauthenticated raw GET to 127.0.0.1:4180 and returns whatever answers. classify_port_owner() (602) accepts any JSON with ok==true, a merely non-empty instanceToken (never compared to this launch's token — the token check authenticates nothing), and a pid. The only guard before /bin/kill -TERM/-KILL (682/697) is pid_is_agent_overlay_server() (620/630), which checks that /bin/ps -o comm= for that pid resolves to filename exactly 'agent-overlay-server'. Critically, the code never verifies that the PID from the health JSON is the process actually bound to port 4180 — it trusts the squatter's self-reported PID and only re-checks its executable name. So a process that wins the bind race on 4180 can return the PID of any legitimate agent-overlay-server (e.g. a dev console on another port), and the desktop app will signal-kill it. The mechanism is exactly as claimed; low severity is appropriate (bounded to processes named agent-overlay-server; on a single-user desktop the squatter already holds kill rights, so real residual risk is a cross-user DoS on a shared host). Cited locations 664/708/602/644 are accurate. | Before signaling, verify the target PID actually owns CONSOLE_PORT rather than trusting the self-reported pid — e.g. resolve the port's listener via lsof -i/-nP or /proc rather than the health-response field, or confirm the socket peer's PID (SO_PEERCRED-style / local port-ownership lookup). Additionally, make the instanceToken check meaningful (compare against a value this launch persisted for its own sidecar) instead of only testing non-emptiness, so a squatter's fabricated token is rejected. At minimum, document and constrain that reap can target any agent-overlay-server PID and add the port-ownership check to close the trust gap. |

| 105 | F:overlay-agent-runner:10 | Fable | Agent-Overlay | crates/agent-runner/src/reconcile.rs:952 | security | low | Confirmed | The cron projection never escapes or rejects `%`, which Vixie crontab treats specially even inside shell single quotes (an unescaped % terminates the command; the remainder becomes the command's stdin with % translated to newlines), so an argument containing `%` (workspace path, --overlay-arg, --runner-command) produces a silently truncated command with attacker-influencable stdin once installed via `cron install` — the crate hardened the systemd path against exactly this (`%` → `%%` in systemd_quote, reconcile.rs:980) and hardened cron against `\n`/`\r`, but left cron's `%` unhandled. | The code confirms the asymmetry the finding describes. `shell_quote` (reconcile.rs:952-962) only single-quotes values containing characters outside `[a-zA-Z0-9_./:=@+-]`; `%` falls into the single-quoted branch and is emitted verbatim — single quotes are shell syntax and do NOT protect `%` from Vixie/ISC crontab's pre-shell parsing, where the first unescaped `%` in the command field is translated to a newline and everything after it is fed to the command as stdin (crontab(5)). By contrast `systemd_quote` (reconcile.rs:964-995) explicitly emits `%%` at line 980 and errors on control chars, establishing the fail-closed posture the cron path lacks. The cron guard `first_line_breaking_cron_value` (388-407) only rejects `\n`/`\r`, never `%`. `render_cron_unit` (459-479) maps every dispatch arg through `shell_quote` and interpolates them verbatim into the `{cron} {runner_command} {quoted_args}` line. Those args come from `dispatch_argv` (836-860): `--workspace <workspace_path>`, `--project`, `--overlay-command`, and repeated `--overlay-arg <arg>` — all config-derived, none screened for `%`. `render_cron_runner_command` (804-818) likewise runs runner-command parts through `shell_quote`. The install flow then writes these fragments verbatim into the live crontab: `install_command` (crontab.rs:158-186) reads the projected fragments and calls `write_live_crontab(state_dir, &proposed)`. So a `%` in a workspace path / --overlay-arg / --runner-command produces a silently truncated command with the remainder redirected to stdin once `cron install` runs. The claim, its evidence, its cited locations, and its low severity are all accurate. | Close the cron `%` gap to match the systemd sibling's fail-closed posture. Preferred, and consistent with the existing cron hardening pattern: extend the pre-render guard (`first_line_breaking_cron_value`, or a renamed `first_unsafe_cron_value`) to also detect `%` in any dispatch arg, runner-command part, or runner_command_line, and skip such triggers with a warning — exactly as `\n`/`\r` are handled today at reconcile.rs:388-407 and 366-374. (Naive escaping is awkward here because `shell_quote` wraps values in shell single quotes, where a `\%` intended for crontab's parser would reach the shell as a literal backslash; if escaping is chosen instead of rejection, it must be validated end-to-end through both the crontab parser and the shell.) Add a regression test alongside `shell_quote_matches_the_ts_rule` covering a `%`-bearing workspace path, and note in the `build_cron_units` doc-comment (352-357) that `%` is rejected for the same crontab-smuggling reason as line breaks. |

## Disputed findings (cross-review stance ≠ plain agreement)

| ID | Stance | Rebuttal argument | Judge's ruling |
|---|---|---|---|
| C:cx-overlay-core:2 | refine | The mechanics are exactly as claimed, but 'high' overstates exploitability because no client-controlled path exists. Confirmed: `find_layered_file` selects the first layer file via `exists()` (memory.rs:236-243) and `read_layered_optional_text` calls `std::fs::read_to_string` with no canonicalization or containment check (memory.rs:220-233), so a symlink at `memory/identity.md` inside a workspace layer would be followed to any readable host file. However, the relative path is never attacker-supplied: sections and entity kinds are closed enums (memory.rs:23-29, 61-66, 78-83) joined to fixed filenames (memory.rs:122, 165-168), so the only actor who can plant the symlink is whoever authors the workspace — largely the same trust domain that defines the policy and shell tools. The sharper framing is an internal-consistency gap: overlay-core's canonical file API DOES enforce post-symlink containment on reads and refuses symlink write targets (workspace_files.rs:310-335 read_optional_contained_text, 340-362 assert_contained_write_target), while the MCP memory resource path bypasses that established convention. Real hardening gap, medium at most (high only if opening untrusted third-party workspaces is an expected use case). | **Needs-fix** — see above |
| C:cx-vault-rust:1 | refine | The code mechanism is accurate: validate_native_chat_config_fields (native_chat.rs:273-289) accepts any http/https base_url and any non-empty env-var name (line 302-314), native_chat_api_key resolves that env var via std::env::var (line 2622) or the keychain, and call_openai_compatible_chat_with_client sends it as `Bearer {api_key}` to the configured URL (line 2210). So a settings writer CAN point the key at an arbitrary endpoint, and can name an arbitrary env var to read. But 'high' overstates it. This is the intended bring-your-own OpenAI-compatible-provider design, and the settings route (/api/native-chat/settings, server.rs:297) sits behind LocalRequestGuardService which enforces the loopback Host allowlist, foreign-Origin CSRF rejection, and application/json content-type — so a remote/browser attacker cannot set these fields. Both provider clients also use redirect::Policy::none() (lines 2190, 2244), so the Bearer token cannot leak via redirect to an unvalidated host. The one genuinely sharp edge is that api_key_env is unconstrained, so a local settings write could exfiltrate an unrelated process-env secret — but that requires control of the loopback-gated settings surface, not a remote path. Correct severity is low/informational hardening, not high. | **Needs-fix** — Code mechanism confirmed exactly as described: native_chat.rs:273-289 constrains base_url only to http/https scheme (host unbounded); :302-314 accepts any non-empty api_key_env; native_chat_api_key (:2622) reads it via std::env::var; call_openai_compatible_chat_with_client (:2210) sends `Bearer {api_key}` to the configured URL. However, both provider clients set redirect::Policy::none() (:2190, :2244), and the settings route (server.rs:297) sits under the whole-app LocalRequestGuardLayer (server.rs:186/217) whose check (origin_guard.rs:172-226) enforces the loopback Host allowlist, foreign-Origin CSRF rejection, and application/json content-type. There is therefore no remote or cross-origin path to set these fields — writing them requires control of the loopback-gated settings surface. The base_url freedom is the intended bring-your-own-provider design. The real residual is that the unbounded api_key_env lets a local settings write read an unrelated process-env secret and ship it to a chosen endpoint. That warrants low/informational hardening, not high severity, and the claim needs the loopback-gating qualification. |
| F:overlay-desktop:4 | refine | Stale references are real but the count is understated: 14 files cite server/features/*, and api.ts plus ViewContext.tsx bring the affected total to 16; deleted @overlay/core references also remain. | **Confirmed** — Opened the cited code. api.ts:1 reads verbatim: "Client for the /api/* contract served by the Node server (server/)." The backend is gone: `ls server` and `ls server/features` both return "No such file or directory"; the live backend is `crates/overlay-console/Cargo.toml`, which exists. `grep -rln "server/features"` under apps/desktop/web/src returns exactly 14 files (run-launch.ts, export.ts, workspace-tail.ts, memory.ts, canonical-files.ts, PingView.tsx, MemoryView.tsx, EvalReportsView.tsx, RunLaunchView.tsx, CanonicalFilesView.tsx, DiagnosticsView.tsx, DashboardView.tsx, ExportView.tsx, TrajectoriesView.tsx) — matching Reviewer A's count. References are present tense: canonical-files.ts:18-21 says "The write goes through the validated @overlay/core core via the server (server/features/canonical-files.ts)." The @overlay/core package is also deleted (`packages/core` absent; no package.json declares it) yet is still cited in canonical-files.ts, memory.ts, and three views. So both halves of the claim (deleted Node server + deleted @overlay/core, present tense, ~14 modules) hold. Reviewer B's refinement is directionally right but conflates categories: ViewContext.tsx:27 references "server/server.ts" (not server/features), and api.ts:1 references "server/" — these are additional stale-server references beyond the 14, not part of the server/features set. That widens the fix surface without falsifying Reviewer A's stated count, which is exact. |
| F:overlay-cli:1 | refine | Full tilde expansion plus resolution is duplicated in two files, but serve_logging.rs duplicates only expansion with resolution performed later; HOME fallback is repeated at all three call sites. | **Confirmed** — All three cited sites verified. init.rs:271-283 resolve_target_path and workspace_discovery.rs:112-125 resolve_input_path implement the same tilde-expansion (`~`/`~/` special-casing, `&input[2..]`, `trim_end_matches('/')`) followed by node_posix_resolve — same algorithm and same "TS resolveInputPath / path.resolve" Node-semantics provenance comment; they differ only in param typing (&Path with inline path_string() vs &str), which is why they weren't already unified. serve_logging.rs:95-103 resolve_log_dir re-implements only the `~`/`~/` expansion, deferring resolution to setup_serve_log at 72-75 — exactly as A stated ("without the resolve step"). The $HOME fallback `std::env::var_os("HOME").map(PathBuf::from)` is independently repeated at init.rs:60-63, serve_logging.rs:59-62, and workspace_discovery.rs:48-50 (default_home_dir). A change to the expansion or fallback semantics in one copy would not propagate to the others. A's location, category, and severity are all correct; B's "refine" restates a point A already made rather than correcting an error. |
| C:cx-arch:2 | refine | The underlying mismatch is real but narrower than claimed, and the doc is not straightforwardly stale about shipped behavior. Confirmed facts: IntegrationScope is a console-local vocabulary (overlay-console/src/integrations.rs:34), enforced by the console-local INTEGRATION_SCOPE_ROUTES table (auth.rs:112-179); auth.rs pulls only overlay_core::internal::constant_time (auth.rs:9) and never loads overlay-core Policy; and the Policy struct (overlay-core/src/schemas/policy.rs:55-71) has no integration-scope concept at all — so build-plan.md:1388-1389 ('authorization derives from overlay-core Policy') and the Guardrail at :1468-1469 ('expressed as Policy scopes, not per-route special cases') use Policy language the wire-level implementation never adopted. However, the same document accurately records the shipped design in its own progress notes: 'enforced from one central route table in the auth layer' (build-plan.md:1434-1435), write scopes 'join the central route table' (:1443-1444), and the genuinely Policy-derived part — sensitive actions gating through approval.required_for at the MCP handler — is real and recorded (:1445-1447). So the corrected finding is: the aspirational framing sentences at :1389 and :1468-1469 are internally inconsistent with the doc's own implementation record and with the code, and should say authorization is expressed as a declared integration-scope vocabulary enforced centrally in the console auth layer, with Policy governing work execution (approvals), not route access. Severity low rather than med — no reader of the full Phase 9 section would come away believing overlay-core Policy performs the route-scope check. | **Needs-fix** — A real mismatch exists, but Reviewer A's framing and severity need correction, so this is Needs-fix rather than Confirmed. Verified against the code: route authorization is enforced by a console-local vocabulary and a hardcoded route table, NOT overlay-core Policy. auth.rs imports only overlay_core::internal::constant_time::constant_time_eq (auth.rs:9) and never loads Policy; the 8-entry INTEGRATION_SCOPE_ROUTES table (auth.rs:112-179) maps method+path to IntegrationScope, matched by required_integration_scope — the literal "per-route special cases" the guardrail disclaims. IntegrationScope is a console-local enum (integrations.rs:33-47), and the Policy struct (policy.rs:53-71) has no integration-scope concept at all. So build-plan.md:1389 ("authorization derives from overlay-core Policy") and :1468-1469 ("expressed as Policy scopes, not per-route special cases") use Policy language the wire implementation never adopted. However, Reviewer A overstates it as uniform staleness: the same doc's progress notes accurately record the shipped design — "enforced from one central route table in the auth layer" (:1434-1435), write scopes "join the central route table" (:1443-1444) — and the genuinely Policy-derived part is real: Policy carries approval.required_for (policy.rs:41-44,67) and sensitive actions gate through it at the MCP handler (:1445-1447). The defect is therefore an internal inconsistency between two aspirational framing sentences and the doc's own implementation record plus the code — not a doc uniformly stale about shipped behavior. Severity is low, not med: a reader of the full Phase 9 section sees the accurate route-table description and would not conclude overlay-core Policy performs the route-scope check. |
| F:overlay-desktop:1 | refine | Predictable /tmp path and no peer/message validation are real, enabling pre-bind launch denial and unauthenticated focus requests, but the only accepted command is show_main_window and blocking lasts only while the squatter maintains the socket. | **Needs-fix** — Code confirms the core defects. single_instance_socket_path() (line 89) builds a predictable world-readable /tmp path. claim_single_instance() treats any successful connect as an existing instance and exits the launch (notify_existing_instance lines 117-123 → return Ok(false) lines 137-139), so a local attacker who pre-binds that path denies every launch; on the sticky-bit /tmp stale-file path, remove_file fails, bind returns AddrInUse, and the function returns Err — launch fails even with no live attacker process (lines 141-172). | C:cx-overlay-app:1 | refine | The mechanics are all confirmed, but the framing and severity need correction. So the quick-capture webview does receive full Operator authority over the HTTP control plane — the underlying issue is real. But Reviewer A's framing is wrong on two counts. | C:cx-overlay-core:4 | refine | Factually correct but overrated at medium; this is a low-severity metadata disclosure. Confirmed: `build_active_policy_resource` serializes the entire `Policy` value (policy.rs:34-38), and `Policy` includes `pub secrets: Vec<SecretDeclaration>` with no serde skip (schemas/policy.rs:69-70). `SecretDeclaration` variants carry only references: an env var name (`EnvSecret.name`), a backend ref string (`RefSecret.name`/`ref` for system-keyring/1password/bitwarden/pass), or an exec command line (`ExecSecret.command`) — see schemas/common.rs:380-417. What leaks to an MCP client is reconnaissance metadata (which secrets exist, which backends hold them, exec command text), which is useful to an attacker only in combination with some other foothold — and the client reading the resource is the same agent the policy already governs and equips. Worth trimming the resource, but it is not a med-severity secret exposure. | **Needs-fix** — Verified in code. build_active_policy_resource (Agent-Overlay/crates/overlay-mcp/src/resources/policy.rs:34-38) serializes the entire policy.value with no field filtering. Policy.secrets (crates/overlay-core/src/schemas/policy.rs:69-70) is `pub secrets: Vec<SecretDeclaration>` marked only `#[serde(default)]` — no skip_serializing — so all declarations are emitted. But SecretDeclaration (crates/overlay-core/src/schemas/common.rs:381-417) variants carry only references: EnvSecret.name (env var name), RefSecret.name/reference (keyring/1password/bitwarden/pass ref strings), ExecSecret.command (command line). No variant stores a resolved value. Resolution occurs separately at tool-exec time (crates/overlay-mcp/src/tools/custom.rs:771-782) and resolved values are scrubbed from outputs (custom.rs:836-842); they never enter the serialized Policy. So the disclosure is real (declarations + backend refs + exec command text leak to the MCP client) but it is reconnaissance metadata, not credential exposure, and the reading agent is the one the policy already governs. Med severity overstates it. |

| F:vault-repo-docs:7 | refine | The prescriptive list at Agent-Vault-Build-Decisions-v1.md:39-51 is stale because it omits active `tools`, `openapi`, `examples`, and `src-tauri` directories, but lines 53-54 only say the former rebuild/export scripts were replaced—not that the entire `tools/` directory was retired; package.json:12-14,26 confirms current tool scripts. | **Needs-fix** — The underlying stale-doc defect is real and confirmed. Agent-Vault-Build-Decisions-v1.md:41-51 gives a prescriptive "Use these top-level directories" list naming only Docs/, vault/, crates/, index/, ops/, web/, tests/ — yet the repo root today also contains tools/, openapi/, examples/, and src-tauri/ (all four verified present via `ls`). tools/ holds three active scripts (build-openapi-docs.mjs, copy-tauri-binaries.mjs, verify-package.mjs) all wired into package.json (lines 12-14, 26). So the list is genuinely stale/incomplete. However, the claim overreaches on one point: Reviewer A says the follow-up sentence "implies tools/ was retired." Lines 53-54 actually read "its rebuild-index / export-context subcommands replace the former `tools/` scripts" — this asserts only that those two specific rebuild/export scripts were superseded, not that the tools/ directory as a whole was retired. Reviewer B's refinement is the accurate reading. The real, confirmed issue is the omission of four active top-level directories from a prescriptive layout list; the secondary "implies tools/ retired" characterization needs correction. |
| F:vault-web-views:6 | refine | metadataForm.tsx:449-451 uniquely uses `window.confirm` and only calls `onInput` when it returns true, but no checked repository file substantiates the asserted silent-accept behavior; Wry uses different platform web engines, so this is an inconsistent, platform-dependent confirmation seam rather than a code-proven fail-open security bug. | **Needs-fix** — Verified at metadataForm.tsx:448–451: the Repair button uses `window.confirm(...)` and only runs `onInput(match.id)` on true. grep across web/src/views and web/src/components confirms this is the sole native confirm() in the frontend; all other confirmable actions use React-state in-app dialogs (e.g. VaultConfirmDialog at VaultsView.tsx:190). So the inconsistency Reviewer A cites is real. But two parts of the claim are unsupported by the code: (1) the "wry silently accepts / fails open" behavior is not substantiated by any repo file (no QA note or code exists for it; grep for wry/window.confirm/silently-accept finds nothing) — it is a platform-dependent assertion, as Reviewer B notes; (2) the security framing is wrong. The gated action, onInput(match.id) at line 450, only sets a form-field value (the same onInput wired to the select at line 419) when exactly one project title matches (titleMatches.length===1, lines 442–455); it resolves an unresolved reference to the obvious target, persists nothing, and the user still reviews and saves. No privilege boundary, data loss, or irreversibility. A genuine low-severity UI-consistency/robustness defect exists, but not the fail-open security bug as claimed. |

## Coverage notes and caveats

Read these before treating the counts above as complete. This was a two-model adversarial audit, and the process itself had gaps worth knowing.

**1. Codex dispatch was flaky under fan-out.** The `codex:rescue` forwarder returns immediately for some runs, handing back a *"task started in the background"* pointer instead of the finished output — so the main workflow judged those areas without waiting for Codex. Concretely:

- **4 of 6 Codex finder runs** (`cx-arch`, `cx-vault-rust`, `cx-overlay-app`, `cx-overlay-services`) went background. Three of them (`cx-arch`, `cx-overlay-services`, `cx-overlay-app`) were recovered from the Codex job registry after the fact, parsed, cross-reviewed by Fable, and judged in a follow-up run — those findings **are** in this report (IDs `C:cx-*`, marked "recovered" in R1-codex.md). `cx-vault-rust` happened to complete in time and was included in the main run.
- Net effect: **all 6 Codex areas are represented.** None were silently dropped.

**2. Twenty Fable arch-group findings were judged without a Codex rebuttal.** The Codex adversarial pass over the `arch` group (2 tasks: `task-mrnq5nbb-v1l3lr`, `task-mrnq8g0i-2i7k0j`) was still running ~50 min after launch (one wedged in "starting") and never returned. Those 20 findings (the `F:arch-docs:*` and `F:arch-vs-*` set) went to the neutral Opus judge **with only Fable's own evidence**, no opposing view. They are marked as `unrebutted` in the stance tally. The judge still independently re-opened each cited file, so they aren't unverified — but they did not get the full two-model adversarial treatment the rest did. If you want that, the two Codex tasks can be polled/cancelled via `/codex:cancel`, or the rebuttal can be re-run.

**3. The verdict distribution should make you slightly skeptical, not comfortable.** 177 Confirmed / 22 Needs-fix / **2 Rejected** is a very low false-positive rate, and only **2 refute** stances came back across 180 rebuttals. That pattern — near-universal agreement — is exactly what you'd expect if the models are *too agreeable* rather than genuinely adversarial. Two mitigating facts: (a) the neutral Opus judge re-verified every finding against the code independently, and it produced all 22 "Needs-fix" corrections (mostly **severity downgrades** — e.g. two "high" symlink/exfiltration findings were knocked to low as defense-in-depth, not exploitable); (b) the 2 rejections are informative — both were **Codex's boldest architectural claims** (that the Overlay→Runner dependency arrow has reversed), and the judge rejected them by grepping the Cargo manifests and finding no such dependency. Treat **high/med security findings as worth acting on**, and **low-severity dead-code/DRY/stale-doc findings as a strong to-do list rather than gospel** — spot-check before bulk action.

**4. Docs skew.** stale-doc is the largest confirmed category (75), concentrated in Agent-Architecture and the repo-level docs. That's the expected shape here — the docs explicitly promise to track code and the code moved through Phase 9 (published integration API, in-process console router, dropped `agent-overlay-server` sidecar, removed Vault PTY) faster than the prose. Most are low-severity line-level corrections; the one **high** (`openapi-contracts.md` telling integrators to fetch the console spec anonymously) is a real integrator-facing trap.

**5. What "Confirmed" means here.** A finding is Confirmed when the Opus judge re-opened the cited `file:line` and the claim held as stated. Needs-fix means the underlying issue is real but the judge corrected the claim, location, or severity (see the corrected text inline in the table and the Disputed section). Rejected means the judge found it false against the code. Nothing in this report was accepted on a reviewer's say-so alone.

**Raw material:** `R1-fable.md` / `R1-codex.md` (independent findings), `X-fable.md` / `X-codex.md` (the point-by-point cross-review), this file (adjudication). Full untruncated per-agent output lives in the workflow journals under `.claude/.../subagents/workflows/`.
